    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="831db94d8c834fd69468642aa1979af4">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '151e9718-45df-4df9-91b4-20f7d798ddbf'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <h1 class="citation__title">Reactive Oxygen-Doped 3D Interdigital Carbonaceous Materials for Li and Na Ion Batteries</h1>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="publicaitonContent-versions"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   <div class="loa comma visible-xs mobile-authors loa-authors-trunc">
      <div class="comma__list"><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Fan/Ling" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="am1" data-db-target-for="am1" aria-controls="am1" aria-haspopup="true" id="am1_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Ling Fan</span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="am1" aria-labelledby="am1_Ctrl" role="region" id="am1">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d129543897" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>Carbonaceous materials as anodes usually exhibit low capacity for lithium ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium ion batteries (SIBs). Oxygen-doped carbonaceous materials have the potential of high capacity and super rate performance. However, up to now, the reported oxygen-doped carbonaceous materials usually exhibit inferior electrochemical performance. To overcome this problem, a high reactive oxygen-doped 3D interdigital porous carbonaceous material is designed and synthesized through epitaxial growth method and used as anodes for LIBs and SIBs. It delivers high reversible capacity, super rate performance, and long cycling stability (473 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>after 500 cycles for LIBs and 223 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 1200 cycles for SIBs, respectively, at the current density of 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), with a capacity decay of 0.0214% per cycle for LIBs and 0.0155% per cycle for SIBs. The results demonstrate that constructing 3D interdigital porous structure with reactive oxygen functional groups can significantly enhance the electrochemical performance of oxygen-doped carbonaceous material.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201600565-sec-0010">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201600565-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Rechargeable lithium ion batteries (LIBs) and sodium ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as two of the most promising electric energy storage systems because of their respective high energy density and low cost.<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0001_R_d129543769e264" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Commun. Nano Energy Science Nat. Commun. Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Sci. ACS Nano">1</a></span> In recent years, many researchers focus on exploring new anode materials with high performance for LIBs and SIBs; for instance, metal oxide,<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0002_R_d129543769e266" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Energy Adv. Mater. Adv. Mater. Adv. Funct. Mater.">2</a></span> sulfide,<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0003_R_d129543769e268" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Nat. Commun. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Funct. Mater.">3</a></span> phosphide,<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0004_R_d129543769e270" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Nanotechnol. ACS Nano">4</a></span> and metal alloy.<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0005_R_d129543769e272" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces ACS Nano">5</a></span> However, their severe volume expansion during charge/discharge process drives researchers to explore nanosized materials to remedy this defect, leading to high cost and limited production. In comparison, organic carbonaceous materials as anodes have more advantages in nontoxicity, structural diversity, large-scale production, and low cost. Hence, more and more researches have been devoted to designing and developing novel carbonaceous anode materials for LIBs and SIBs.<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0006_R_d129543769e275" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Nano Energy">6</a></span></p>
         
         <p>As we know, heteroatom doped carbonaceous materials usually exhibit attractive electrochemical performance, such as enhanced electric conductivity and capacity. The enhanced electrochemical performance is mostly due to the heteroatom atoms in the carbonaceous materials which could generate extrinsic defects and provide additional reaction sites.<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0007_R_d129543769e279" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Carbon">7</a></span> Up to now, carbonaceous materials doped with nitrogen atom (N) or sulfur atom (S) are mostly investigated and have achieved significant progress.<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0007_R_d129543769e281" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Carbon">7</a>, <a href="#smll201600565-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0008_R_d129543769e281" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Mater. Chem. A Nat. Commun. Chem. Commun. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater. Sci. Rep. Carbon Adv. Mater. Electrochim. Acta Carbon Carbon">8</a></span> Compared with other heteroatom atoms, the oxygen element is very common in organic materials, especially for the carbohydrate and carbonyl compounds. More importantly, the reactive oxygen groups could afford extra capacity through the transform of carboxide and enolate. While most of the reported oxygen-doped carbonaceous materials exhibit unsatisfactory electrochemical performance.<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0009_R_d129543769e283" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Solid State Electrochem. J. Solid State Electrochem. Int. J. Miner., Metall. Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Int. J. Energy Res. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></span> Therefore, it is necessary to find a way to improving the electrochemical property of the oxygen-doped carbonaceous materials.</p>
         
         <p>In addition to the chemical content in the electrode materials, the structure of the electrode materials could also significantly affect its electrochemical performance. It is believed that carbonaceous materials with 3D porous structure have more advantages for batteries application, because electrodes with 3D porous structure could provide more reaction sites and reduce the diffusion distance of alkaline ions, leading to superior electrochemical property. For example, 3D graphene has been extensively investigated in batteries application due to its high surface areas and excellent electronic conduction.<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0010_R_d129543769e288" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Energy Environ. Sci. Nat. Commun. Adv. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Nature Nat. Commun. Nanoscale">10</a></span></p>
         
         <p>Herein, we propose a facile method through pyrolysis organic materials with multi-carbonyl to in-situ synthesize reactive oxygen-doped carbonaceous materials as anode materials for LIBs and SIBs. Interestingly, when using 500 °C as the pyrolysis temperature, the synthesized carbonaceous material exhibits unique 3D interdigital porous structure. Using it as anode for LIBs and SIBs, it delivers a high reversible capacity of 790 and 505 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, excellent rate performance, and long cycling stability of 473 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles and 223 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 1200 cycles for LIBs and SIBs, respectively, at the current density of 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (with a capacity decay of 0.0214% per cycle for LIBs and 0.0155% per cycle for SIBs). This paper demonstrated that constructing electrodes with reactive oxygen-doped 3D interdigital porous structure could significantly enhance the electrochemical performance of oxygen-doped carbonaceous material.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201600565-sec-0020">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201600565-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussions</h2>
         
         <p>The oxygen-doped carbonaceous materials were synthesized facilely by epitaxial growth method along with thermal polymerization of perylene-3,4,9,10-tetracarboxylic acid dianhydride (PTCDA) compound (as illustrated in <b>Figure&nbsp;</b><a href="#smll201600565-fig-0001">1</a>a). The PTCDA monomers in the substrate lead to the formation of oriented crystallites growing.<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0011_R_d129543769e319" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Cryst. Growth">11</a></span> Then the film was connected to each other with the coupled carbonyl groups to form the stable 3D interdigital porous structure. The polymerization temperature (500 °C) is based on the thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) of pure PTCDA (Figure&nbsp;S1, Supporting Information) to avoid the significant weight loss and the excessive functional groups decomposition.[<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0009_R_d129543769e321" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Solid State Electrochem. J. Solid State Electrochem. Int. J. Miner., Metall. Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Int. J. Energy Res. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></span>]</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201600565-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c2da888b-3a21-4b0a-ba79-39a3ed353af0/smll201600565-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c2da888b-3a21-4b0a-ba79-39a3ed353af0/smll201600565-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f16237b3-f145-4881-ac20-99bc105eb05c/smll201600565-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c2da888b-3a21-4b0a-ba79-39a3ed353af0/smll201600565-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201600565-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201600565" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration for preparation of 3D OC-500. b–d) TEM of OC-500. e–h) EDS elemental mapping of OC-500. i) High resolution TEM of OC-500.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The morphology of as-prepared OC-500 was characterized by transmission electron microscope (TEM), as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0001">1</a>b–d. Generally, the OC-500 material consists of many interdigital porous arrays. Furthermore, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0001">1</a>b reveals a thin layer region of this material, from which we could visually observe the minimum thickness of the interdigital arrays in the OC-500, about 10 nm. This unique cross-linked interdigital structure could afford a stable framework and is beneficial for the electron transfer in the material. Moreover, as shown in Figure&nbsp;S2a (Supporting Information), this material also has many cross-linked spaces, indicating this interdigital carbonaceous material has a 3D porous structure. This unique 3D interdigital porous structure can not only fully infiltrate by the electrolyte to provide transport channels for Li<sup>+</sup> or Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, but can also afford enough space to accommodate the volume change during charge/discharge process. The energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) elemental mapping (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0001">1</a>e–h) exhibits the homogeneous distribution of oxygen (Figure&nbsp;1g) and carbon (Figure&nbsp;1h). While the high resolution TEM (HRTEM) (shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0001">1</a>i) exhibits no obvious lattice, indicating the 3D OC-500 is amorphous. And this disordered nature could be further verified by the polycrystalline rings of the selected area electron diffraction pattern (Figure&nbsp;S2b, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>To explore the forming mechanism of this 3D interdigital porous structure, the TEM of pure PTCDA and other pyrolysis temperature (300, 700, 900 °C) was also performed. As shown in <b>Figure&nbsp;</b><a href="#smll201600565-fig-0002">2</a>a, the pure PTCDA was organic quantum dots with a very small size. When the pyrolysis is 300 °C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0002">2</a>b), the PTCDA quantum dots were agglomerated together to form a certain scale of channel structure. With the pyrolysis temperature raised to 500 °C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0002">2</a>c,d), the 3D porous interdigital structure was formed and this structure could be maintained even when the temperature rose to 700 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0002">2</a>e) and 900 °C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0002">2</a>f).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201600565-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/47e08c66-9369-4520-bb95-74b0ef4a109c/smll201600565-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/47e08c66-9369-4520-bb95-74b0ef4a109c/smll201600565-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/94d11372-6511-4eab-a8df-ebf0314967b5/smll201600565-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/47e08c66-9369-4520-bb95-74b0ef4a109c/smll201600565-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201600565-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201600565" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">TEM images of prepared organic carbon materials at different pyrolysis temperature. a) Pure PTCDA. b) OC-300. c,d) OC-500. e) OC-700. f) OC-900.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further identify the structure of as-prepared 3D interdigital OC-500, X-ray diffraction (XRD) measurement was performed. Figure&nbsp;S3 (Supporting Information) shows the XRD patterns of 3D OC-500 and pure PTCDA. XRD analysis demonstrates that the PTCDA used in this study has a <i>β</i>-crystalline form.<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0012_R_d129543769e391" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Mater.">12</a></span> While the OC-500 exhibits similar XRD pattern with PTCDA except for a proportion of <i>α</i>-crystalline and a broad peak at 24.6°–26.2°. And the humpy peak at 24.6°–26.2° is (002) diffraction of disordered carbon, consistent with the HRTEM.</p>
         
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) measurement was performed to analyze the chemical states of C and O in the OC-500. As shown in <b>Figure&nbsp;</b><a href="#smll201600565-fig-0003">3</a>a, two main peaks at 293.2 and 537.7 eV are corresponding to the C1s and O1s, respectively. For the high-resolution C1s curve (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0003">3</a>b), four peaks at binding energies of 284.8, 286.1, 288.8, and 290.1 eV are indexed to sp<sup>2</sup> carbon atoms (C<span class="icomoon"></span>C or C<span class="icomoon"></span>H), C<span class="icomoon"></span>OH, CO and OC<span class="icomoon"></span>O, respectively. For the O1s spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0003">3</a>c), two intensively peaks at 532.3 and 534 eV are contributed to CO and C<span class="icomoon"></span>O, respectively. Besides, in order to further investigate the chemical states of oxygen in the material, Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) and Raman spectra were performed. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0003">3</a>d, the peaks of C<span class="icomoon"></span>OH (3426 cm<sup>−1</sup>), CO (1735 cm<sup>−1</sup>) and <span class="icomoon"></span>CO<span class="icomoon"></span>O<span class="icomoon"></span>CO (1015 cm<sup>−1</sup>) could be verified by the FTIR spectra.[<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0009_R_d129543769e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Solid State Electrochem. J. Solid State Electrochem. Int. J. Miner., Metall. Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Int. J. Energy Res. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a>, <a href="#smll201600565-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0013_R_d129543769e417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Phys. Chem">13</a></span>] Besides, Raman spectra (Figure&nbsp;S4, Supporting Information) also confirmed the peaks of CO (1299 cm<sup>−1</sup>), disordered carbon (D-band, 1369 cm<sup>−1</sup>), and crystalline graphene (G-band, 1571 cm<sup>−1</sup>).<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0014_R_d129543769e427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">14</a></span> To obtain an insight into the porous structure of 3D OC-500, we measured the BET surface area and the pore diameter of the 3D OC-500 using nitrogen adsorption and desorption isotherms. As shown as Figure&nbsp;S5a (Supporting Information), the Brunaer-Emmet-Teller (BET) surface area of the OC-500 is only 3.93 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, the pore diameters of the 3D OC-500 are around 27–57nm (Figure&nbsp;S5b, Supporting Information). The large pore diameter in the 3D OC-500 is beneficial for the infiltration of electrolyte.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201600565-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1c22b873-3af9-40cf-8220-0a063930871f/smll201600565-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1c22b873-3af9-40cf-8220-0a063930871f/smll201600565-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/27a40804-c1c4-452b-b7fc-d218197d4295/smll201600565-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1c22b873-3af9-40cf-8220-0a063930871f/smll201600565-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201600565-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201600565" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XPS survey spectrum of 3D OC-500. High-resolution XPS spectrum of OC-500 for b) C1s and c) O1s. d) FTIR spectrum of OC-500.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>For the purpose of investigating the electrochemical performance of as-prepared 3D interdigital carbonaceous material, the electrodes were used for LIBs and SIBs to investigate their lithium and sodium storage properties, respectively.</p>
         
         <p><b>Figure&nbsp;</b><a href="#smll201600565-fig-0004">4</a>a–e shows the first and second discharge/charge curves of synthesized organic carbonaceous materials at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. For the electrode of pure PTCDA, OC-300, OC-500, OC-700, and OC-900, an initial discharge/charge capacity of 1423/455, 1715/515, 1111/824, 791/556, and 550/405 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained, corresponding the initial Coulombic efficiency (CE) of 32%, 30%, 74%, 70%, and 73%, respectively. The very low CE for pure PTCDA and OC-300 is due to their imperfect morphology and structure defects, which result in much more solid-electrolyte interphase (SEI) formed and more insertion of Li<sup>+</sup> into the defects, leading to very low reversible capacity. While for the OC-500, OC-700, and OC-900, the initial CE are more than 70% because of their unique 3D interdigital porous structure. Moreover, the capacity of these materials in high voltage platform (over 1.0 V) was increased from pure PTCDA to OC-300, and reduced from OC-500 to OC-900, this is due to the activation of the carbonyl functional groups from pure PTCDA to OC-300 and the reactive oxygen functional groups reduced sharply due to the decompose of oxygen functional groups when the temperature rose over 500 °C. Besides, the OC-500 exhibits the highest reversible capacity compared with others, this is due to the OC-500 which possesses the unique 3D porous structure as well as more reactive carbonyl functional groups (O weight content: 14%). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0004">4</a>f shows the Nyquist plots of these organic carbonaceous electrodes. It clearly reviews that the charge-transfer impedance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) on electrode–electrolyte interface (semicircle) was reduced with the pyrolysis temperature rise from 300 to 500 °C, and there is no significant change for OC-500, OC-700, and OC-900 but with an increasing Li<sup>+</sup> diffusion in the solid state electrodes from OC-500 to OC-900. This phenomenon is mostly due to the formation of 3D interdigital porous structure and the decomposition of oxygen-functional groups in high temperature. This interdigital structure and reduced oxygen-functional groups could provide a fast electron transfer channel and reduce the SEI impedance.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201600565-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/deea05d7-3cd6-4d66-96a2-c22a969f03dc/smll201600565-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/deea05d7-3cd6-4d66-96a2-c22a969f03dc/smll201600565-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8b78f117-b30a-4cbb-af8b-a1d43066edb2/smll201600565-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/deea05d7-3cd6-4d66-96a2-c22a969f03dc/smll201600565-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201600565-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201600565" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The discharge/charge curves of LIBs at a current density of 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. a) Pure PTCDA. b) OC-300. c) OC-500. d) OC-700. e) OC-900. f) Nyquist plots of fresh LIBs.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>For further investigate the lithium storage property of 3D OC-500 electrode, cyclic voltammograms (CV) curves, rate, and cycling performance were performed. <b>Figure&nbsp;</b><a href="#smll201600565-fig-0005">5</a>a reveals the charge/discharge curves of 3D OC-500 at the current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The initial discharge and charge capacity of 3D OC-500 electrode are 1111 and 812 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to an initial CE of 73.1%. The irreversible capacity loss is mostly due to the formation of SEI<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0015_R_d129543769e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Chem. Mater.">15</a></span> and the partial irreversible lithium extraction process. Besides, the charge/discharge curves reveal three main platforms after the second discharge process. The high voltage region (1.2–2.0 V) and the middle voltage region (0.1–1.2 V) are related to the reaction between covalently O with Li<sup>+</sup>. While the low voltage region (&lt;0.1 V) is account for the insertion of Li<sup>+</sup> into condensed carbon. The charge/discharge platforms were verified by the CV measurement (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0005">5</a>b). According to the first CV curve, four cathodic peaks could be observed at 2.02, 1.41, 0.32, and 0.01 V. The 0.01 V is attributed to the Li<sup>+</sup> insert into condensed carbon. The peak at 2.02 and 1.41 V can be interpreted as the reactions between covalent O with Li<sup>+</sup>. While the peak at 0.32 V is due to the reaction between covalent O with Li<sup>+</sup> and the formation of SEI. While two main anodic peaks at 1.76 and 2.26 V are corresponding to the Li<sup>+</sup> deintercalation from covalent O, consistent with the initial charge/discharge process. Moreover, apparent differences are observed from the first CV curve compared with later curves, mainly because of the formation of SEI layer in the first cycle. Besides, the cathodic peak of 0.32 V in the first cycle transfer to 0.6 V in the subsequent cycles, agree well with the charge/dischargecurves.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201600565-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fd7bccce-a306-4f6e-8d36-8a32edd06762/smll201600565-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fd7bccce-a306-4f6e-8d36-8a32edd06762/smll201600565-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/91513baa-9e18-4598-b14c-2092bc1a37c9/smll201600565-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fd7bccce-a306-4f6e-8d36-8a32edd06762/smll201600565-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201600565-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201600565" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Lithium storage property of 3D OC-500 anode. a) The charge/discharge profiles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. b) CV curves at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. c) Rate property at various current densities from 100 to 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Cycling performance at the current density of 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>This 3D OC-500 electrode not only delivers high reversible capacity, but also exhibits excellent rate performance and superior cycling stability for LIBs. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0005">5</a>c presents the rate performance of 3D OC-500 at various current densities from 100 to 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The electrode delivers a stable reversible capacity of 686, 624, 522, 456, 413, 394 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at the current of 100, 200, 400, 600, 800, 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. When the current density returns to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the 3D OC-500 electrode recovers a reversible capacity of 650 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, demonstrating an outstanding rate recyclability. Considering that this 3D OC-500 exhibits excellent rate performance, we select a high current density of 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> to investigate its cycling stability at large current density. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0005">5</a>d, the first discharge and charge capacity are 787 and 544 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to an initial CE of 69.1%. Furthermore, a relative high reversible capacity of 473 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> still remained even after 500 cycles with capacity retention of 89.3% (0.0214% decay per cycle) based on capacity of the second cycle. This superior Li<sup>+</sup> storage performance of 3D OC-500 is due to its unique 3D interdigital porous structure and high reactive oxygen functional groups. Up to now, such a high reversible capacity along with long cycling stability was rarely reported in organic-based carbonaceous materials for LIBs application.[<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0008_R_d129543769e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Mater. Chem. A Nat. Commun. Chem. Commun. J. Mater. Chem. A Adv. Sci. Adv. Energy Mater. Sci. Rep. Carbon Adv. Mater. Electrochim. Acta Carbon Carbon">8</a>, <a href="#smll201600565-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0016_R_d129543769e568" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nano Energy Chem. Commun. Adv. Mater.">16</a></span>]</p>
         
         <p>In addition to its excellent lithium storage property, this 3D OC-500 also exhibits superior sodium storage performance (<b>Figure&nbsp;</b><a href="#smll201600565-fig-0006">6</a>a–e).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201600565-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/4ac3660e-2360-4f0c-be0f-eb10168b8c04/smll201600565-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/4ac3660e-2360-4f0c-be0f-eb10168b8c04/smll201600565-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4cfe4cd9-545e-4d5f-841f-a1198965ebec/smll201600565-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/4ac3660e-2360-4f0c-be0f-eb10168b8c04/smll201600565-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201600565-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201600565" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Sodium storage property of 3D OC-500 anode. a) The discharge profiles at 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. b) CV curves at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup>. c) Rate property at various current densities from 100 to 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. d) Cycling performance at a current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Cycling performance at the current density of 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Similar to LIBs, the discharge curves of SIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0006">6</a>a) also exhibit three main voltage profiles. The voltage region of 1.2–2.0 V, 0.1–1.2 V is related to the reaction between covalently O with Na<sup>+</sup>, and the voltage region below 0.1 V is due to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into condensed carbon. Interestingly, an upward trend of platform and capacity could be observed at high discharge profile voltage, similar to other reports.[<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0007_R_d129543769e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Carbon">7</a>-<a href="#smll201600565-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0009_R_d129543769e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Solid State Electrochem. J. Solid State Electrochem. Int. J. Miner., Metall. Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Int. J. Energy Res. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a>, <a href="#smll201600565-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0017_R_d129543769e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Energy">17</a></span>] We interpreted this phenomenon as the activation of the C<span class="icomoon"></span>O bond to CO bond in the 3D OC-500 electrode. To learn more about the sodium storage mechanism of 3D OC-500 electrode, CV measurement (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0006">6</a>b) was performed. According to the subsequent CV curve, the 3D OC-500 electrode exhibits two main redox couples located at 1.44/1.83 V and 1.92/2.30 V, which could be account for the reactions between Na<sup>+</sup> with covalent O.[<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0009_R_d129543769e622" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Solid State Electrochem. J. Solid State Electrochem. Int. J. Miner., Metall. Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Int. J. Energy Res. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a></span>] The other cathodic peak at around 0.01 V is due to the Na<sup>+</sup> insert into condensed carbon. While the cathodic peak at 0.23 V in the first cycle transferred to 0.6 V in the subsequent cycles is due to the reaction between covalent O with Li<sup>+</sup> and the formation of SEI in the first cycle, similar to LIBs.</p>
         
         <p>The rate performance and cycling stability of 3D OC-500 electrode for SIBs were further investigated. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0006">6</a>c exhibits the rate performance of 3D OC-500 electrode at various current densities from 100 to 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The 3D OC-500 electrode delivers a high stable capacity of 422, 372, 342, 308, 283, and 263 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> at the current of 100, 200, 400, 600, 800, and 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Notably, even at the current density of 2000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, a capacity of 210 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> (44.6% retention normalized to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) still can be maintained. Besides, When the current rate returns to 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>, the 3D OC-500 electrode recovers a reversible capacity of 420 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>(nearly no capacity loss), demonstrating its outstanding high rate recyclability for SIBs. Using the current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0006">6</a>d), the electrode delivers an initial discharge and charge capacity of 766 and 483 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to an initial CE of 63.1%. And the 3D OC-500 electrode exhibits superior stable recyclability for 100 cycles with capacity retain of 90.1% based on capacity of the second cycle. Moreover, a high current density of 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup> was used to further investigate its cycling stability. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0006">6</a>e, the curve reveals the initial discharge and charge capacity of 441 and 299 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to an initial CE of 67.8%. Furthermore, a reversible capacity of 223 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was still maintained even after 1200 cycles (0.0155% decay per cycle) with capacity retention of 81.4% based on capacity of the second cycle. Considering the capacity contribution from acetylene black, the cycle performance of pure acetylene black was also performed for LIBs and SIBs (Figure&nbsp;S6, Supporting Information). It is obviously that the acetylene black only contribute a little capacity for LIBs and SIBs compared with 3D OC-500. The lithium and sodium storage property of this 3D OC-500 is very competitive with other reported heteroatom (O, N, S) doped organic carbonaceous materials (Table S1, Supporting Information).[<span><a href="#smll201600565-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0007_R_d129543769e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater. Energy Environ. Sci. Carbon">7</a>-<a href="#smll201600565-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0009_R_d129543769e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - J. Solid State Electrochem. J. Solid State Electrochem. Int. J. Miner., Metall. Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Int. J. Energy Res. Energy Environ. Sci.">9</a>, <a href="#smll201600565-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0017_R_d129543769e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Energy">17</a>, <a href="#smll201600565-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll201600565-bib-0018_R_d129543769e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Adv. Energy Mater. Nano Lett. Nano Energy J. Am. Chem. Soc.">18</a></span>]</p>
         
         <p>For comparison, the sodium storage performance of the pure PTCDA, OC-300, OC-700, and OC-900 was also investigated. <b>Figure&nbsp;</b><a href="#smll201600565-fig-0007">7</a>a shows the relationship between initial discharge curves of SIBs and the electrodes pyrolysis at different temperatures. Interestingly, nearly no discharge capacity was found over 1.0 V for pure PTCDA, C-700, and C-900. This is because of the carbonyl groups in the pure PTCDA could not afford fast discharge progress. For C-700 and C-900, the reason is the high pyrolysis temperature have decomposed the carbonyl functional groups, result in no more active reaction sites. For C-300 and C-500, they deliver capacity of 97 and 54 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> due to the uniform morphology and residual carbonyl functional groups, respectively. Furthermore, the capacity beneath 1.0 V of these electrodes is also connected with the oxygen functional groups. For pure PTCDA, C-700, and C-900 they exhibit nearly the same capacity of 276, 255, and 284 mA h g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. While for C-300, a higher capacity of 339 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained due to the partial reactive O reaction sites. Moreover, a highest capacity of 639 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> was obtained for OC-500, this is because of its unique 3D interdigital porous structure and more O reaction sites. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll201600565-fig-0007">7</a>b shows the discharge capacity of pure PTCDA, OC-300, OC-700, and OC-900 at the current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. The pure PTCDA and OC-300 have lower capacity due to their imperfect structure, while the OC-700 and OC-900 exhibit also the same capacity due to their unique 3D structure without O reactive sites.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll201600565-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e74e61bf-dda0-4371-ad32-4f98453d1b1b/smll201600565-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e74e61bf-dda0-4371-ad32-4f98453d1b1b/smll201600565-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6ba3716f-efa7-4869-a6ae-210d08a6e0b9/smll201600565-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e74e61bf-dda0-4371-ad32-4f98453d1b1b/smll201600565-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll201600565-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.201600565" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The initial discharge curves of pure PTCDA, OC-300, OC-500, OC-700, and OC-900 at the current density of 50 mA g<sup>−1</sup>. b) The discharge capacity of pure PTCDA, OC-300, OC-700, and OC-900 at the current density of 100 mA g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>For insight into the sodium storage mechanism of 3D OC-500, we performed the CV measurement using the battery after hundreds discharge/charge processes. As shown in Figure&nbsp;S7 (Supporting Information), it is very interesting that the redox couples at 1.44/1.83 V and 1.92/2.30 V in the initial several cycles were disappeared, and only one main redox couple at 1.12/1.88 V was observed. We infer that this is due to the electrochemical self-assembly of CO bond and C<span class="icomoon"></span>O bond in the 3D OC-500, leading to formation of one kind of uniform reactive covalent O. The uniform reactive covalent O resulting in only one redox couple was observed in the CV curve for SIBs, which is consistent with the discharge curve of SIBs at the 50th cycle.</p>
         
         <p>The excellent electrochemical performance of 3D OC-500 as anode material for LIBs and SIBs is due to its unique morphology and structure, which could be attributed to the following reasons. On one hand, the unique 3D porous interdigital structure could afford enough spaces for volume expansion during lithiation/sodiation processes. Furthermore, as shown in Figure&nbsp;S8 (Supporting Information), after reduplicative delithiation/desodiation processes, the interdigital structure still could be maintained to some extent without pulverization. On the other hand, the Li<sup>+</sup> or Na<sup>+</sup> could react with covalent O (Figure&nbsp;S9, Supporting Information) and insert into the condensed carbon, leading to high capacity. Therefore, combination with the unique 3D interdigital structure to afford fast electron transfer and high reactive oxygen functional groups to afford capacity, a fast rate and stable cycling performance is obtained.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201600565-sec-0030">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201600565-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, we have successfully synthesized 3D interdigital organic carbonaceous materials for energy storage. Benefiting from its unique 3D interdigital porous morphology and high reactive oxygen functional groups, this 3D OC-500 electrode exhibits excellent performance for both LIBs and SIBs. It delivers a high reversible capacity, excellent rate capacity, and superior cycling stability (473 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 500 cycles for LIBs and 223 mA h g<sup>−1</sup> after 1200 cycles for SIBs, respectively, at the current density of 1000 mA g<sup>−1</sup>). This work demonstrated that enhanced electrochemical performance could be obtained for oxygen-doped carbonaceous materials by constructing 3D porous structure with reactive oxygen functional groups.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll201600565-sec-0040">
         
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll201600565-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p><i>Synthesis of Carbonaceous Materials</i>: PTCDA was purchased from J&amp;K Scientific, and used as received. The 3D interdigital carbonaceous material was obtained from a facile method of thermal polymerization of PTCDA. Typically, PTCDA was homogeneously placed into a quartz boat, then annealing in Ar atmosphere at 500 °C for 4 h with a heating rate of 2 °C min<sup>−1</sup>. For comparison, different pyrolysis temperature of 300, 700, and 900 °C were selected to pyrolysis PTCDA. The obtained black powders (named as pure PTCDA, OC-300, OC-500, OC-700, and OC-900 according to the pyrolysis temperature) were used as active materials directly.</p>
         
         <p><i>Material Characterizations</i>: TGA measurement of pure PTCDA was conducted on a Perkine Elmer TGA-7 with a heating rate of 10 °C min<sup>−1</sup> under N<sub>2</sub> atmosphere at a range from 30 to 800 °C. The microstructure morphology of organic carbonaceous materials was studied by JEOL JEM-2100F TEM. The crystal structure of the OC-500 powder and the pure PTCDA powder was characterized by XRD (Philips, X'pert pro) using Cu Kα radiation (<i>λ</i> = 0.154056 nm) over a 2<i>θ</i> range of 5–80. XPS was performed by ESCALAB 250Xi. Raman spectra of OC-500 and pure PTCDA powder were tested by a Renishaw 2000 system, FTIR curves of OC-500 and pure PTCDA powder were performed by WQF-510A.</p>
         
         <p><i>Electrochemical Measurements</i>: The pure PTCDA, OC-300, OC-500, OC-700, or OC-900 was mixed with acetylene black and carboxyl methylated cellulose at a weight ratio of 7:2:1. Then the mixture was dissolved into the C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>5</sub>OH:H<sub>2</sub>O (1:2.5, w/w) and stirred for 8 h before pasted onto a thin copper foil and dried at 70 °C in vacuum oven. The average weight of the active material was about 1.0–1.2 mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. For the assembling of LIBs, lithium foil was used as the counter electrode, 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> LiPF<sub>6</sub> in ethylene carbonate/diethyl carbonate (1:1, v/v) was used as the electrolyte. For SIBs, sodium foil was used as the counter electrode, 1 <span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> in ethylene carbonate/dimethyl carbonate (6:4, v/v) was used as the electrolyte. All the cells were assembled with 2032 type coin cell in glovebox under Ar (H<sub>2</sub>O &lt; 0.5 ppm, O<sub>2</sub> &lt; 0.5 ppm). The galvanostatic charge/discharge process was investigated by Arbin Instrument BT 2000 model at various current densities at voltage range of 3.0–0.01 V (vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li or vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na), all the capacity were calculated by the weight of active materials. CV curves were recorded ranged 3.0–0.01 V (vs Li<sup>+</sup>/Li or vs Na<sup>+</sup>/Na) at a scan rate of 0.1 mV s<sup>−1</sup> using the electrochemical workstation. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were performed with an AC voltage of 5 mV at a frequency range of 0.01–100 kHz.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
